From raanders at acm.org  Mon Apr 14 15:00:37 2008
From: raanders at acm.org (Roderick A. Anderson)
Date: Mon, 14 Apr 2008 15:00:37 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Postfix mystery...
In-Reply-To: <1208208314.27057.15.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <1208207246.27057.5.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>	<8bbd30b30804141411w3755d535wfffe0a8dcbb7d77@mail.gmail.com>
	<1208208314.27057.15.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <4803D405.4020301@acm.org>

robinson-west user wrote:
> On Mon, 2008-04-14 at 14:11 -0700, Andreas Turriff wrote:
>> Postfix documentation points to this function:
>>
>> *smtpd_reject_unlisted_recipient (default: yes)*
>>
>> Request that the Postfix SMTP server rejects mail for unknown recipient
>> addresses, even when no explicit
>> reject_unlisted_recipient<http://www.postfix.org/postconf.5.html#reject_unlisted_recipient>access
>> restriction is specified. This prevents the Postfix queue from
>> filling up with undeliverable MAILER-DAEMON messages.
> 
> Okay, but now why did putting this in 
> smtpd_recipient_restrictions make a difference for sqlgrey???
> I'm guessing that it's the default, but that it isn't applied
> before sqlgrey is if it isn't explicitly stated.  I wish it 
> was in my book...  Is there a newer syntax I should be using???
> I'm hoping to keep the greylist from filling up with bogus
> email attempts that will poison the whitelist.

There are a lot of "smtpd_recipient_restrictions" that _CAN_ be used but 
if you don't tell postfix to use them then it won't. 
"smtpd_recipient_restrictions" are applied in the order you list them so 
by putting this before the greylisting it gets applied first.

That make any sense?  Did to me!

On some mail spoolers I support we go even farther to cut-the-crap out.

         reject_unauth_pipelining,
         reject_non_fqdn_sender,
         reject_non_fqdn_recipient,
         reject_unknown_recipient_domain,
         reject_unknown_sender_domain,
         reject_unlisted_recipient,
         permit_mynetworks,
         ...

We typically get a 75-80+ percent rejection rate and the only customers 
that have complain are those on yahoo-groups and that was for 
header_checks that tossed messages with six or more of the same 
character in a row in the Subject.


Rod
-- 
> 
> smtpd_recipient_restrictions =    reject_non_fqdn_recipient,
>                             reject_unknown_recipient_domain,

On Mon, 14 Apr 2008, Rich Shepard wrote:

> On Tue, 8 Apr 2008, Paul Heinlein wrote:
>
>> It sounds like her machine is sending jobs directly to the printer, not to
>> another computer acting as a print server.
>
>   Exactly.
>
>> What does the DeviceURI setting look like in /etc/cups/printers.conf?
>
>   DeviceURI socket://lj5:9100
>
>   Same as on the server and other hosts. But, when I (for example) 
> send a job to the printer and it's off, it stacks up in the print 
> queue. The jobs from her machine used to do that, too.

My understanding is that cups will try to spool to /var/spool/cups (or 
its moral equivalent on your wife's computer). That directory is 
usually owned by user root and some printing group (lp, sys, or 
whatever). It holds a tmp directory that's group-writable.

You might check permissions on those directories; perhaps they've 
gotten too tight or your wife isn't a member of some necessary group.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/

From heinlein at madboa.com  Tue Apr 15 09:18:54 2008
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Tue, 15 Apr 2008 09:18:54 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Negative namespace deltas
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.1.10.0804150909520.26432@guild.galois.com>

This is an "Ask Slashdot" sort of question:

   What are your best practices for maintaining negative
   namespace deltas?

I maintain the typical sort of system namespaces -- DNS hostnames and 
cnames, DHCP MAC tables, e-mail aliases -- along with what I imagine 
are more atypical ones like Xen virtual hosts and Trac (wiki) 
instances.

Adding to those namespaces is a clear process: someone submits a 
ticket and I push the relevant bits into production.

Subtracting from those namespaces is murkier. People don't always tell 
me when a system has retired or an e-mail alias is obsolete. Those 
namespace entries lurk like so much kruft in my config files.

What are the best methods you've developed for identifying negative 
deltas and getting the appropriate customer to approve the change?

Thanks.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/


From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Tue Apr 15 09:35:56 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Tue, 15 Apr 2008 09:35:56 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Negative namespace deltas
In-Reply-To: <alpine.OSX.1.10.0804150909520.26432@guild.galois.com>
References: <alpine.OSX.1.10.0804150909520.26432@guild.galois.com>
Message-ID: <20080415163556.GB4412@sentinelchicken.org>

Hello Paul,

On Tue, Apr 15, 2008 at 09:18:54AM -0700, Paul Heinlein wrote:
> This is an "Ask Slashdot" sort of question:
> 
>    What are your best practices for maintaining negative
>    namespace deltas?
> 
> I maintain the typical sort of system namespaces -- DNS hostnames and 
> cnames, DHCP MAC tables, e-mail aliases -- along with what I imagine 
> are more atypical ones like Xen virtual hosts and Trac (wiki) 
> instances.
> 
> Adding to those namespaces is a clear process: someone submits a 
> ticket and I push the relevant bits into production.
> 
> Subtracting from those namespaces is murkier. People don't always tell 
> me when a system has retired or an e-mail alias is obsolete. Those 
> namespace entries lurk like so much kruft in my config files.
> 
> What are the best methods you've developed for identifying negative 
> deltas and getting the appropriate customer to approve the change?


This is a good question.  While I haven't had to deal with this issue
specifically related to the configuration elements you listed, I have
worked in security departments where the issue was about user accounts.
The same problem comes up with contractors and "process" accounts
(network accounts assigned for daemons and other automated processes).

Managers are all too eager to get an account set up for what ever their
project is, but they are very bad at asking those accounts to be
disabled when they are done.  Since this does have a significant
security impact over time, the approach to deal with it was somewhat
heavy handed (which may not be possible in your case).  Any account
granted in certain classes (essentially for non-employees) must have an
expiration date.  Managers are notified shortly before that date to
ensure they don't need the account any more.  If not, or if there is no
response, the account is disabled.

Of course this tends to anger lots of managers, but the trade off is
reasonable.  In your situation, it may not be as reasonable for most of
the configurations you listed.  However, for others, such as firewall
rule changes and external DNS configurations, it might be reasonable to
have expiration dates.  Perhaps if someone needs a permanent change with
no expiration, a management person higher up in the food chain should be
required to authorize it. 

Not a great answer, but hope that gives you some ideas.
tim


From srau at rauhaus.org  Tue Apr 15 12:09:02 2008
From: srau at rauhaus.org (Stafford A. Rau)
Date: Tue, 15 Apr 2008 12:09:02 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] What's Wrong With This IP Address?
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0804131008180.29366@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0804131008180.29366@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <20080415190902.GB7848@igor.rauhaus.net>

* Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> [080413 10:14]:
 
> [rshepard at salmo ~]$ whois 116.71.190.196
> Unknown AS number or IP network. Please upgrade this program.
> 
>    According to the IANA page I found via Google, 116/8 is assigned to apnic.
> 
>    Why can't I get ISP information for this address?

In addition to the other suggestions, you may need to explicitly specify
the whois server from which to query. Your client might not be smart
enough to know that you're looking for a network object rather than a
domain object.

Try the same request thusly:

whois -h whois.arin.net 116.71.190.196

--Stafford

From krisa at subtend.net  Tue Apr 15 15:29:26 2008
From: krisa at subtend.net (Kris)
Date: Tue, 15 Apr 2008 15:29:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Negative namespace deltas
In-Reply-To: <alpine.OSX.1.10.0804150909520.26432@guild.galois.com>
References: <alpine.OSX.1.10.0804150909520.26432@guild.galois.com>
Message-ID: <48052C46.1060601@subtend.net>

Paul Heinlein wrote:
> I maintain the typical sort of system namespaces -- DNS hostnames and 
> cnames, DHCP MAC tables, e-mail aliases -- along with what I imagine 
> are more atypical ones like Xen virtual hosts and Trac (wiki) 
> instances.

For MAC addresses you could pull the MAC tables on your switches and put them
into a db.  Same goes for IP addresses from the ARP tables on your routers; you
could then even associate the two to get MAC-IP pairings for historical analysis.

Once you had this data you could use it to audit DNS.  If an IP hadn't been seen
in the db reports for X days/months, flag it for removal via some sort of
notification.  Or transverse your zone files checking it against the last
timestamp of the IP addresses in your database.

Email aliases are a bit tricky, as most would always have spam coming to it, and
might not necessarily be used for outgoing email.

For aliases that having outgoing email, you could script up something to search
logs for outgoing email.

At PSU we had some crusty perl called macmon and arpmon which would scoop up all
the MAC tables and router ARP tables to show all IP/MAC use on campus.  This was
pushed into mysql which another system (ipdb) used to manage IP space (static
assignments, jack #'s, switchport info).

I pushed for collaboration between PSU and OSU to revamp the system using OSU's
Maintain project in 2004, but it never gained a foothold.  You might check it out:

http://maintainproject.osuosl.org/

-- 
I'm just a packet pusher.

Message-ID: <8663uhiudx.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Larry" == Larry Brigman <larry.brigman at gmail.com> writes:

Russell> Please take a moment to point out your favorite
Russell> rs232-serial-to-usb converter cable (and/or device).  Note I
Russell> am talking about "regular" rs232, not TTL voltages.  Must
Russell> have good linux support.  There is a bonus for a device not
Russell> based on the pl2303 chip, since I've already got one of those
Russell> in the system I am designing and I'd like to be able to
Russell> distinguish them easily.  A model number and your
Russell> vendor/source would be helpful as well.  Additional bonuses
Russell> for: inexpensive (< $25); and local sources.

Larry> Devices with FTDI chipset.  These include Keyspan and others.
Larry> I plugged a Keyspan USB to serial device into one of our Redhat
Larry> 9 boxes and it showed up and loaded the correct drivers without
Larry> prompting.  I haven't found one locally but I do have a link.

Thanks for you input.  I ended up grabbing a couple random ones at ENU
for under $20 that claimed linux support.  They turned out to use
pl2303 parts.  In order to distinguish the two devices, I am
standardizing the hub ports utilized (e.g. gps goes in hub port 2, the
line to the instrumentation goes in hub port 3) and then getting the
associated /dev/ttyUSB<n>'s by pawing through /sys.

From daniel_petcher at yahoo.com  Wed Apr 16 14:09:42 2008
From: daniel_petcher at yahoo.com (Daniel Petcher)
Date: Wed, 16 Apr 2008 14:09:42 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] LPI certification through free geek...
In-Reply-To: <1208365922.19373.5.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <114129.35783.qm@web55614.mail.re4.yahoo.com>

PCC Capital Center is located just south of the Tannasborne retail
complex... at 185th and Walker Rd.

In addition to Prometric testing, this campus hosts classes from
Portland Community College, Oregon Graduate Institute, Oregon Health
Sciences University and several other public institutions of higher
learning.

More exciting still, it hosts the Capital Career Center where you can
find dozens of free services to help build your job search skills. I
highly recommend Becky Meier's High-Tech Networking groups on Wednesday
and/or Friday mornings as a place where you can go to meet people who
may know a thing or two that can give you an advantage with regard to
your next prospective employer. They've got free classes in resume and
letter writing, interview skills, and programs to help overcome all
sorts of challenges (career path change, recent incarceration, senior
citizen, recent graduate, limited English skills, HIV/AIDS, presently
employed, long absence from the world of work, Windows user, etc.) and
find satisfying work. They are also good at helping "just plain folks,"
whatever that may mean to you.

They even have a resource room where you can use freely use their
Internet-connected computers for your job search. It never hurts to get
another pair of eyes to examine your resume, and all of their services
are free to the job seeker. (Grant money from folks interested in
seeing Oregonians employed pays for it all.)

They have been invaluable to me in my job search activities for several
years now.

-dP

From znmeb at cesmail.net  Wed Apr 16 20:12:44 2008
From: znmeb at cesmail.net (M. Edward (Ed) Borasky)
Date: Wed, 16 Apr 2008 20:12:44 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] OT: Geeky Portland Recommendations
In-Reply-To: <48069DE4.4050309@dpape.com>
References: <48069DE4.4050309@dpape.com>
Message-ID: <4806C02C.80702@cesmail.net>

Daniel Pape wrote:
> - what broadband service is best?
Depends on what your needs are. The "big three" are Verizon, Comcast and 
Qwest. I have Comcast and have no plans to switch. Then again, I'm not 
into digital TV/movies/music, etc.

> - what cellular company?
Best coverage in the Portland area, Verizon. If you have an iPhone, 
you're locked into them. I have Verizon but I'm seriously considering an 
iPhone when the next versions come out. I don't know about the others.

> - good stores for computer parts/supplies?

Fry's if you don't mind driving to Wilsonville and back. It's not 
bicycling distance from downtown.


>favorite beer?

When I could drink beer, I never could pick a favorite. There are so 
many local beers of every recipe, including some recipes that were 
invented here, that you could drink a different one every day of your 
adult life.

A couple of other notes: Tri-Met plus bicycles is a great way to get 
around. Gasoline prices tend to be a tad higher here than some other 
parts of the world for a couple of reasons.

There are dozens of open source users' groups here. PLUG you know about, 
but there are one or two other Linux groups, a strong Ruby brigade, a 
functional languages group, a strong PostgreSQL group and quite a few 
others. Check out http://calagator.org (and if you're a Rails hacker, 
you can help out.) :)

Oh, yeah ... do you play "Werewolf?"

independent coffee shops. The walk to the brew-pub is more on the

From dwight at dwightandamy.com  Thu Apr 17 15:40:47 2008
From: dwight at dwightandamy.com (Dwight Hubbard)
Date: Thu, 17 Apr 2008 15:40:47 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] OT: Geeky Portland Recommendations
In-Reply-To: <48069DE4.4050309@dpape.com>
References: <48069DE4.4050309@dpape.com>
Message-ID: <321871a60804171540jc08ce94sa9c95694d658cfa4@mail.gmail.com>

On Wed, Apr 16, 2008 at 5:46 PM, Daniel Pape <dpape at dpape.com> wrote:
>  - what broadband service is best?

I use Spirit One for my DSL service and I've had very good luck and
service from them.  On the other hand some of the Condos we've been
looking at were already wired for FiOS high speed internet which is
probably the fastest option in the area.

>  - what cellular company?

I currently have AT&T/Cingular and though the coverage they have is
pretty good here, I've had consistent issues with calls not going
through (I.E. getting a all lines are busy message when trying to make
a call).  I've also had Sprint when I first got here, they didn't have
as much coverage but when I had coverage their service worked well.

That said, it seems like all the Cellular companies suck in some way
shape or form.  If you already have cell service it might be easier to
just keep using it instead of paying for new service.

>  - cafes/bars with wifi

No idea, I'm in the suburbs and most independent coffee places have
free wifi.  However, the coverage at least out on the south side is
sparse enough that I normally use my cell phone to connect my laptop
to the internet.

>  - good stores for computer parts/supplies?

There are some local large chains like PC Club and Frys that have tons
of computer parts and supplies.

-- 
Dwight Hubbard (RHCE, VCP)
dwight at dwighthubbard.com
http://computing.dwighthubbard.info

jjj at Devil7:~$ dmesg |tail
[21583.078610] usb 2-1: new full speed USB device using uhci_hcd and address 3
[21583.104052] usb 2-1: configuration #1 chosen from 1 choice
<extraneous stuff deleted>

jjj at Devil7:~$ lsusb
Bus 007 Device 001: ID 0000:0000  
Bus 006 Device 004: ID 04b3:4485 IBM Corp. 
Bus 006 Device 001: ID 0000:0000  
Bus 001 Device 005: ID 0483:2016 SGS Thomson Microelectronics Fingerprint Reader
Bus 001 Device 004: ID 0a5c:2110 Broadcom Corp. 
Bus 001 Device 001: ID 0000:0000  
Bus 003 Device 001: ID 0000:0000  
Bus 005 Device 001: ID 0000:0000  
Bus 004 Device 001: ID 0000:0000  
Bus 002 Device 003: ID 07b4:020d Olympus Optical Co., Ltd Digital Voice Recorder VN-240PC
Bus 002 Device 001: ID 0000:0000  

I've googled and haven't found any solutions. Any ideas on how to mount the thing?

page. With the recorder plugged in I get:

jjj at Devil7:~$ odvr
Model: 4100PC

That's really grand. I already knew it was a 4100PC. I unplugged it and
plugged it back in, but still no automount, and dmesg / lsusb still say
the same things.

Any ideas how to use it?

then plug the device in

Just got a reply from the developer. It is not a driver that allows the
device to be mounted. It's a command-line tool that just goes out and
downloads files from the device. The instructions are at "odvr -h." I
had already recorded a couple short files and when I tried to download
them I got an error message that the quality was not supported. And if
it had actually downloaded the files it doesn't show what the filename
is or where it plans to put it.

Tomorrow the POS goes back to the PSU bookstore. Now I'm off to google
for something that actually works on Linux. If anyone knows of any
digital voice recorders that are known to automount, please let me know.

Subject: [PLUG] wireless takes over in Ubuntu Gutsy

Both the built-in ethernet and wirelss devices work fine on my Clevo 
M570ru laptop. Well, almost. No matter how i configure the Network 
Settings applet, both the ethernet and wireless are on and active by 
default. Alarmingly often (though not always), the machine will intially 
connect via the lan (DHCP from my DSL modem), but after a while the 
wireless autoconnects to a neighbor's network, and my poor box is 
confused & can't connect to anything - it has a new ip address, but 
connections time out.

I can disable the wireless connection and "/etc/init.d/networking 
restart" and all is well, but that's annoying.

If, upon login, i manually disable the wireless connection via the 
Network Settings applet, all is well, but that's annoying too.

i found one old post in the Ubuntu Forums that describes the problem 
exactly (aside from hardware), but it got no responses. 
http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=125046

Any suggestions on either preventing this, or disabling the wireless 
adapter except when i need it?

Joe

On Wed, Apr 16, 2008 at 5:46 PM, Daniel Pape <dpape at dpape.com> wrote:

> Hi everyone,
>
> My family and I are moving to Portland at the end of April. I've been
> lurking on the list for a few months to get an idea of what's going on.
>
> Now that the time is getting closer, I wanted to ask if people would be
> so kind as to offer me a few recommendations for things like:
>
> - what broadband service is best?


I use QWest DSL with SpiritOne as the provider.  Hevanet is also good.


> - what cellular company?


I use Qworst.  Don't make that same mistake.  They sell old cutout bin
phones for full retail prices and claim it is a bargain.  I will probably
move to T-Mobile when I can get out of my contract.


> - cafes/bars with wifi


There are lots of them downtown.  Jax and Paddys both have wifi.  Advanced
topics used to meet at Paddy's.  (We out grew them.) We are now at Jax.


> - computer repair shops?


I have not needed one in a long time.  Usually by the time they need repair,
and I can't do it, the machine is old enough that I just replace it
outright.


> - good stores for computer parts/supplies?


I usually go to Pacific Solutions.


> - anything else? favorite beer? best indian restaurant? :)


For beer, you need to visit "The Horse Brass" on 52nd(?) and Belmont.  Many
many beers, hard cider on tap, and good British pub food.  It will be even
better starting the first of the year when all bars go non-smoking.

For Indian food, I usually do the all-you-can eat lunch at India House
downtown.


> thanks. I'm looking forward to meeting people at the PLUG meetings.
>

See you there.

From creswick at gmail.com  Fri Apr 18 13:41:32 2008
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Fri, 18 Apr 2008 13:41:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] wireless takes over in Ubuntu Gutsy
In-Reply-To: <4808C301.2050402@easystreet.net>
References: <4808C301.2050402@easystreet.net>
Message-ID: <c834ec80804181341l5b47eafn798766d947eb74c1@mail.gmail.com>

On Fri, Apr 18, 2008 at 8:49 AM, Joe Shisei Niski
<joeniski at easystreet.net> wrote:
>
>  Any suggestions on either preventing this, or disabling the wireless
>  adapter except when i need it?

If the networks it's picking up are not conflicting (that is, as long
as they use different IP spaces, like 192.168.1.x and 192.168.0.x)
then you could almost certainly solve this problem by specifying a
route (with 'route').

I'm afraid I can't really help with the specifics though, maybe:

# assuming your wired 'net is on eth0:
$ route add default dev eth0

# see if that did something:
$ netstat -rn

that *may* break things horribly, but a reboot should set it all back
to normal... The netstat -rn command alone may be enough to clarify
what's going on, and make a solution more obvious.  (You'll probably
want to run that before and after any route tweaking you do.)

--Rogan


From johnxj at comcast.net  Fri Apr 18 14:07:54 2008
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Fri, 18 Apr 2008 14:07:54 -0700

On Fri, 18 Apr 2008 13:39:33 -0700
"Roderick A. Anderson" <raanders at acm.org> dijo:

> Has anyone tried or succeeded at getting a bluetooth headset to work 
> with a soft phone?
> 
> In my case I'm working with CentOS 5.

Not exactly with a cell phone (never tried the phone), but I do have
bluetooth headphones working flawlessly in Gutsy x86_64. I had a lot of
trouble until I installed Blueman, a GUI sync program. Blueman is not
in the repos, and I can't remember the URL, but you can download it.

Whenever anyone has trouble with bluetooth the first thing I recommend
is Blueman.

Since posting that I have discovered that the Zoom H2 is reputed to
work well with Linux. It uses a removable SDRAM chip. However, the
company says it only supports Windows and MacOS. It does save in MP3
and WAV formats, though. The big drawback is the cheapest you can find
it for is ~ $150. Plus it has too many features. Very bewildering for
those of us who are gadget-challenged.

> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

Go with sqlgrey and the php interface to it.

List the relay recipients explicitly amd
make sure you use reject_unlisted_recipient 
in recipient restrictions or your whitelist 
will fill up with junk.  I use postgresql
with it.  I had problems getting greylist.pl
to work at all, that's why I went to sqlgrey.

     Michael C. Robinson

Message-ID: <48099ADB.1040902@verizon.net>

I just performed the automatic upgrade from gutsy to hardy on my ubuntu 
box. Compared to some previous ubuntu upgrades (like from dapper to 
edgy) this one was a snap. The only problems I ran into were due to my 
own arrogance. I chose to keep my original menu.lst and xorg.conf files 
instead of letting the updater handle them automatically. This 
(partially) broke my system and I tried repairing them manually but had 
to end up letting the automated system recreate the defaults (like it 
wanted too in the first place). Once I did this then ubuntu worked 
flawlessly again and I was able to easily rewrite the modifications I 
had been trying to keep.


Do not be afraid to joust a giant just because some people insist on believing in windmills.

> smtpd-policy/greylist.pl that comes with postfix
> SQLgrey
> greylistd

> Does anyone have any strong opinions about these?  This is for a small
> office with < 20 active email users.

Galen,

   I tried greylist.pl and greylistd, but was never happy with either one. At
first, in ignorance, I invoked the greylisting too early in the mail
checking sequence. Ergo, _every_ message was grey listed and nothing came
through. I suffled them down the sequence but never came up with a
satisfactory solution.

   Whatever I tried seemed to require more attention and maintenance on my
part than the potential benefit it provided.

   After removing them and spending some time learning how to tune
SpamAssassin I achieved the results I still see: perhaps 1-3 spam per day
coming in. Of course, I have extensive postfix UCE rule lists and use
several RBLs for checking.

YMMV, as it is written,

Rich

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Apr 19 08:28:38 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 19 Apr 2008 08:28:38 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] preferred greylisting software for postfix?
In-Reply-To: <1208581398.6916.75.camel@simplicity.buscaluz.org>
References: <48095AE2.1060901@seitzassoc.com>
	<9511DFB1-06C1-4B22-9561-E93FE7F2CB00@gmail.com>
	<1208581398.6916.75.camel@simplicity.buscaluz.org>
Message-ID: <20080419152838.GA5016@gate.kl-ic.com>


Another vote for postgrey.  It stops perhaps 98% of the incoming
crud before spamassassing has to deal with it (and postfix on my
Mail Transport Agent firewall machine is getting about 1500 spam
attempts per hour).  

There is one postfix/firefox enhancement that I would like; perhaps
it is already out there and I haven't found it yet.

Some websites have some kind of email confirmation - "enter your
email address into this form and we will continue doing business
after you confirm an email we will send to you".

If the web page is a domain that postgrey hasn't seen before, it 
may be an hour before I actually see the email confirmation.  Unless
I add that domain to /etc/postfix/postgrey_whitelist_clients.local
on my MTA/firewall machine before I click "send" on the web form.
For me, that is an ssh login and a text edit.  Annoying.

Wouldn't it be cool if firefox had a plugin that recognized that
my email address was being sent out on a form, and it automagically
sent the domain up to the MTA/firewall to get added to the whitelist
(after a confirmation popup with the proposed domain in an edit
window)?  Besides the plugin, that would also require some sort
of listening process running on the firewall.  These two bits of
software are a bit beyond my meager programming ability.  

That would remove the last minor annoyance of greylisting for me.
Postgrey doesn't have it, but nobody else does either, AFAIK.  

Keith

I've _heard_ port 25 outbound is blocked, but I can't confirm it. That
would be a real bummer for those using STARTTLS and authentication,
since it typically still listens on port 25. Looks like Google lets
you send to their Gmail relay on 465 or 587.

underground services all this week rolling out FiOS in my neck of the
woods:

Though I have only good things to say about my current ISP, Spiritone,
I'm tempted to jump ship for 15 Mbit symmetric. Wish I could keep them
as the ISP and just have Verizon for the "local loop".

On Sat, 19 Apr 2008 09:02:01 -0700
Denis Heidtmann <denish at dslnorthwest.net> dijo:

> John Jason Jordan wrote:
> > On Fri, 18 Apr 2008 13:00:31 -0700 (PDT)
> >...
> > Since posting that I have discovered that the Zoom H2 is reputed to
> > work well with Linux. It uses a removable SDRAM chip. However, the
> > company says it only supports Windows and MacOS. It does save in MP3
> > and WAV formats, though. The big drawback is the cheapest you can find
> > it for is ~ $150. Plus it has too many features. Very bewildering for
> > those of us who are gadget-challenged.

> I know nothing about recorders, but I know that I record from records onto the 
> computer using Audacity.  (Do I recall you bad-mouthed Audacity in the past?). 
> I believe you can restrict the bandwidth and the # channels.  Maybe a script 
> could be generated to launch things in the right configuration.

No, I often good-mouth Audacity. I use it a lot for phonetics work
because it has the wonderful capability of slowing down speech without
changing the pitch. Even Praat can't do that (although Praat is the
tool to use if you need spectrograms).

However, this problem is completely different. I need a simple digital
voice recorder which also functions as a USB mass storage device so I
can pull the recordings off the device and put them on my computer. The
Olympus VN series uses a crackpot way of recording that makes this
impossible. First, the recordings are saved on the device as DSS files,
but you cannot access them that way. Instead, you must install their
proprietary software which knows the signature of the device, looks for
it on the USB bus, then downloads the files while converting them to
WMA. The software is the only thing that can see the files on the
recorder. And the software runs only on 32-bit XP and Vista. It won't
run on Windows 2000, any 64-bit Windows, or any version of MacOS. How
lame is that? What was so hard about just making it a USB mass storage
device so any computer could see the files? What was the point of going
to the expense of writing their own proprietary software, and then
limiting their market to just 32-bit Windows users?

Once I acquire a device from which I can pull the files I'll probably
use Audacity to play them back, especially if they're WMAs. That is, I
think Audacity can play WMAs; if not I know how to convert them at the
command line.

Meanwhile, there is a VN4100PC on its way back to Olympus. Maybe if
enough disgusted customers send them back they'll catch on.

It costs $150 because it's worth it.  It's a serious recorder with a great
sound, and capable of 90, 120, and 180 degree separation for L-R recording.
Heck, you can even record quad if you want.

I'm using it a lot for my podcasts now.  I even use it as my "desk" mike for
voiceovers/intros/outros, because it'll go "live" over the USB link and look
like a mike to the OS.

However, the default setting is trivial, and works.  Flip it on, hit the
record button twice.  Hit the stop button when you're done.

>>>>> "John" == John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> writes:

John> But having said that, your recommendation makes me want to put the H2
John> back on the list. Tell me, how good is the microphone at picking up
John> voice over a distance, e.g., in a lecture hall?

It will sound like what it sounds like to your ears.  So, it can't remove the
mud from multipath sound, but if you can hear it, you can record it with the
H2.  I've never had such a clean sound before.

For some samples, you can download a few of the last dozen podcasts from
http://podcast.insightcruises.com/.  Everything from the "Scientific American"
cruise published so far was recorded with my H2.  (A few of the upcoming shows
required me to use a backup recording from Steve Mirsky's H4, for reasons that
are embarassing. :) Keep in mind that I'm recording in fairly hostile
situations (such as, the speaker has a mike, but I'm just recording the
ambient room and not tied into the audio system), and I'm compressing the
sound a bit to keep my download bandwidth within reason.  If you ask nicely, I
can forward you a snippet of the original recording.

From krisa at subtend.net  Mon Apr 21 17:47:37 2008
From: krisa at subtend.net (Kris)
Date: Mon, 21 Apr 2008 17:47:37 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Debian djb
Message-ID: <480D35A9.5090409@subtend.net>

I've been a fan of djb software for quite some time.  I used qmail from
1999-2002 before moving on to postfix, and djbdns from 1999-present.

The new DPL says next stable release is a goal for 2008.  Maybe my time enjoying
Ubuntu 8.04 will be less than I thought.

Debian Project News seems to be kicking again, and this gem was in it:


Debian Lenny to be shipped with qmail and djbdns in main
--------------------------------------------------------
In the past Debian users who favoured using the popular software from
Dan J. Bernstein (like qmail or djbdns) needed to use packages shipping
only the source and compile these programmes them self or use packages
provided by external services. This was due to a restrictive license
used by him to distribute his software. However, since Bernstein
decided to change the licence[sic] terms for his software, qmail and djbdns
may be distributed in Debian main and already efforts are going on to
provide packages with the next stable release.

-- 
I'm just a packet pusher.

Try this:

Go into System > Administration > Software Sources

Somewhere in the dialog box that opens there is an option to add the CD
drive to the available places (like adding it as an additional
repository). I can't tell you exactly where it is because I have Gutsy
and the appearance of the dialog box is slightly different.

If that doesn't work, you can get the same options from within
Synaptic, Settings > Repositories.

If that doesn't work you can add it manually to the /etc/sources.list
file. Afterwards you have to refresh the repository list. I don't know
how to add it (the syntax for adding the CD - probably something
like /dev/CDROM). I also can't remember the command to refresh the
repository list, but someone else here will chime in I'm sure.

If none of that gets it seeing the CD-ROM, then it's time to search the
Ubuntu forums.

Subject: [PLUG] apache rewrite

I can't seem to find the magic incantation to enable rewrite in 
apache.  I have the following in my /etc/httpd/conf/http.conf file:

...
LoadModule rewrite_module modules/mod_rewrite.so
...
<VirtualHost *:80>
     ServerAdmin webmaster at foo.bar
     DocumentRoot /var/www/external/html
     ServerName www.foo.bar
     ErrorLog logs/www-error_log
     CustomLog logs/www-access_log combined

     RewriteEngine On
RewriteLog /tmp/rewrite-www.log
RewriteLevel 9
     RewriteCond %{HTTP_HOST}    !^www\.foo\.bar [NC]
     RewriteCond %{HTTP_HOST}    !^$
     RewriteRule           ^/(.*) http://www.foo.bar/$1 [L,R]
</VirtualHost>


I don't even get a rewrite log file with this, so I clearly have 
something basic wrong.  The rewritecond and rewrite rule were copied 
out of the apache docs.  This is apache 2.2.3 on CentOS 5.1.

The application of a cluestick would be much appreciated.  I've wasted 
far too much time on this.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Apr 23 01:03:41 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 23 Apr 2008 01:03:41 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Hardy Heron, Xorg7.3, and Projectors
Message-ID: <20080423080341.GA21158@gate.kl-ic.com>


I downloaded the Ubuntu 8.04LTS Release Candidate (Hardy Heron) (*)
Live CD and tried it out.

Hardy uses X.org 7.3 for display.  One of the nice features is that
it GETS PROJECTORS RIGHT.  For distros from about the last 1.5 years,
you had to reboot the computer to make it see an external display -
it was not plug-and-go, the way it was 2 years ago before HAL.

You can either select a fixed external display configuration
with "Applications/Other/Screens_and_Graphics", or you can call
Preferences/Screen Resolution from the System menu, and set the
appropriate resolution for the external projector (or whatever)
and PRESTO,  the screen is cloned to the external display without
rebooting.   I hope we can try a few different laptops with my
projector and the Hardy live CD at the next Advanced Topics meeting.

Keith

(*) Note: in preparation for downloading the final iso image with
rsync.  I'm hoping that an rsync download onto a mostly correct RC
iso will be a lot faster than a virgin download of the final.  I'll
keep you folks posted on how the speeds compare when I try this next
weekend.

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

	<86fxtgn5pz.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>
	<20080420092501.cdbdbf4c.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <867ieok9ja.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>

>>>>> "John" == John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> writes:

>> For some samples, you can download a few of the last dozen podcasts from
>> http://podcast.insightcruises.com/.  Everything from the "Scientific American"
>> cruise published so far was recorded with my H2. 

John> The sound in those recordings was plenty clean enough. How far away
John> were you from the speaker?

It varied.  For the Steve Mirsky talk, I had stupidly placed the H2 on the
podium, but directly behind where his laptop was sitting, so there's no direct
sound path from him to the H2... just the multipath from the room audio.

For the talk with Dr Gaby, she was walking around on a wireless mike, and I
had just set the recorder on a table at the front of the room.  Again, no
direct audio path - just picking up what you would have heard had you sat
there.  I think it was near her laptop though, so you could sometimes hear her
moving to the laptop to change screens or type something.

That's what I'm liking about this recorder.. it seems to be very much a great
set of virtual ears... whatever you would have heard sitting somewhere, you
can record.  It's absolutely a bargain at this pricepoint, and very portable
as well.

On Tue, 22 Apr 2008, Galen Seitz wrote:

> I can't seem to find the magic incantation to enable rewrite in
> apache.  I have the following in my /etc/httpd/conf/http.conf file:
>
> ...
> LoadModule rewrite_module modules/mod_rewrite.so
> ...
> <VirtualHost *:80>
>     ServerAdmin webmaster at foo.bar
>     DocumentRoot /var/www/external/html
>     ServerName www.foo.bar
>     ErrorLog logs/www-error_log
>     CustomLog logs/www-access_log combined
>
>     RewriteEngine On
> RewriteLog /tmp/rewrite-www.log
> RewriteLevel 9
>     RewriteCond %{HTTP_HOST}    !^www\.foo\.bar [NC]
>     RewriteCond %{HTTP_HOST}    !^$
>     RewriteRule           ^/(.*) http://www.foo.bar/$1 [L,R]
> </VirtualHost>
>
>
> I don't even get a rewrite log file with this, so I clearly have 
> something basic wrong.  The rewritecond and rewrite rule were copied 
> out of the apache docs.  This is apache 2.2.3 on CentOS 5.1.

If you've got SELinux enabled, Apache won't write to /tmp without a 
policy adjustment. Put your rewrite log in /var/log/httpd instead.

Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/

BTW, the rsync commands I used were:

(1) Listing the PSU Hardy repository:
rsync -v rsync://mirrors.cat.pdx.edu/ubuntu-releases/.pool/ubuntu-8.04*

(2) Using rsync to update the file:
rsync -v rsync://mirrors.cat.pdx.edu/ubuntu-releases/.pool/ubuntu-8.04-rc-desktop-i386.iso ubuntu-8.04-rc-desktop-i386.iso

What I expect to do if I use rsync:
  cp  ubuntu-8.04-rc-desktop-i386.iso  ubuntu-8.04-desktop-i386.iso 
  rsync -v rsync://mirrors.cat.pdx.edu/ubuntu-releases/.pool/ubuntu-8.04-desktop-i386.iso ubuntu-8.04-desktop-i386.iso

I did something similar from the beta to the r.c ;  at 400KB/sec
download, it took about 10 minutes to replace about half the image.
I imagine on Hardy release day, that will be quite a bit slower.
So if there is a quick set of user level commands to update it with
jigdo, I will be a happy camper. 

BTW, I run cloned RHEL5 on all my machines, but I spread Ubuntu among
people with less specific needs, and help Ubuntu users at the clinic,
hence the desire for Hardy.

Keith,
    Sorry, I should have mentioned that Jigdo doesn't work for the 
'desktop' images because of the way they are packaged. It only works for 
the Alt/Server disks. The 'desktop' disk basically consists of a few 
files and then a giant squashfs file. This file contains the live disk 
and all of the .deb files needed for the install. Jigdo works by 
comparing the individual .deb files on the disk with the files on the 
server using the .jigdo file as a reference. Since there are no 
individual .deb files on the 'desktop' disk, you are out of luck. A 
similar thing is probably going to happen why trying to rsync the 
desktop iso. I'm betting it is going to want to get all of the squashfs 
file, but I've never tried it. I'd be interested to know how it works.
> Note that the purpose of the exercise is to build an exact binary 
> image of the official Ubuntu ISO when I am done.  Will Jigdo do this,
> or will there be small differences, and the published MD5 checksum 
> for that ISO will be different than my image?
>   
Jigdo will do this, just not for the desktop disk. It will create you a 
disk that is identical to the real iso, it just does it on a file by 
file basis. I know when I did this in the past, the difference between 
the beta releases was only a handful of files, so it was lightning quick.
> I did something similar from the beta to the r.c ;  at 400KB/sec
> download, it took about 10 minutes to replace about half the image.
>   
Ok, so it rsync can see the differences, even with the squashfs image. 
Good to know. I'll keep that in mind when it comes time to update.
> I imagine on Hardy release day, that will be quite a bit slower.
> So if there is a quick set of user level commands to update it with
> jigdo, I will be a happy camper. 
>   
A tutorial for the whole Jigdo process is available at [1]. 
> BTW, I run cloned RHEL5 on all my machines, but I spread Ubuntu among
> people with less specific needs, and help Ubuntu users at the clinic,
> hence the desire for Hardy.
>
>   
Ubuntu is definitely more desktop friendly. I use CentOS on most all of 
my servers, and Ubuntu on all of my desktops/laptops.

[1] https://help.ubuntu.com/community/JigdoDownloadHowto

rm -- --one-file-system

Most core utilities that could be confused by names starting with a dash
allow you to use -- as a separator between options and arguments.

Many programs reinvent the option parsing wheel.  I didn't think of ./
but I think that would be my standard way.  Still though API's often
give you easier access to edge cases with shell parsing.  For example
filenames with whitespace can be a pain.

more fun examples
echo stuff > --
$ rm -- --
or
$rm -i -- --
or
$rm -i ./--
echo stuff > -i
$ rm -i -- -i
rm: remove regular file `-i'? y

From robert.citek at gmail.com  Thu Apr 24 06:41:57 2008
From: robert.citek at gmail.com (Robert Citek)
Date: Thu, 24 Apr 2008 08:41:57 -0500
Subject: [PLUG] Deleting strange file name...
In-Reply-To: <20080423232138.pp3ya4wem80kwsw0@web.robinson-west.com>
References: <20080423232138.pp3ya4wem80kwsw0@web.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <4145b6790804240641u70c1bc27p681a29e35f31cd46@mail.gmail.com>

On Thu, Apr 24, 2008 at 1:21 AM, someone <plug_1 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> I accidentally trying to tar one file created a file --one-file-system.
>
>  How do I delete without having rm think I'm specifying an option???

Another trick that I've used is to let the shell tell me the file name
by expanding all the files onto the command line and then editing the
command for the files I want.  For example, let's say your directory
looks like this:

$ ls -1
--one-file-system
a
b
c
|
|foo

Yes, that's a vertical bar (i.e. pipe) in the last two files.  Start
typing this:

$ echo rm ./

Then press the escape key followed by the asterisk:

$ echo rm ./<ESC>*

Your command line should now look like this:

$ echo rm ./--one-file-system ./a ./b ./c ./\| ./\|foo

Edit the line to create the desire command:

$ rm ./--one-file-system ./\| ./\|foo

Under Ubuntu, bash gives you some hints to try if it doesn't
understand the command:

$ rm --one-file-system
rm: unrecognized option `--one-file-system'
Try `rm ./--one-file-system' to remove the file `--one-file-system'.
Try `rm --help' for more information.

Either 'rm --help' or 'man rm' on Ubuntu will give you this:

<quote>
       To remove a file whose name starts with a '-', for example
'-foo', use one of these commands:

              rm -- -foo

              rm ./-foo
</quote>

Good luck and let us know what works for you.

Regards,
- Robert

Thanks for the details, Garl.  I just finished using my "rsync
overwrite the rc" method to update Heron. It took less than 5
minutes.  Probably not as fast as optimized Jigdo, but a lot
less strain on PSU's network than a full download.

Here is the command I used:
$ rsync -v rsync://mirrors.cat.pdx.edu/ubuntu-releases/.pool/ubuntu-8.04-desktop-i386.iso ubuntu-8.04-desktop-i386.iso
ubuntu-8.04-desktop-i386.iso

sent 188420 bytes  received 150809993 bytes  595654.49 bytes/sec
total size is 733079552  speedup is 4.85

The md5sum was the same 8895...f1f1f .  It worked!  Now to burn a
bunch of Heron CDs.  That is a big image, I hope it fits.  I must
make sure that multisession is turned off in K3B when I do this.

# from Fred James
# on Thursday 24 April 2008 11:48:

>Tried "rm -i"? - as in "rm -i *"

The shell expands the glob and passes it to the program.  Thus, if 
anything in ./ starts with '-', it is likely to be seen as an option 
and complained about unless you use the form `rm -i -- *` or 
`rm -i ./*`

--Eric

* someone <plug_1 at robinson-west.com> [080423 23:22]:
> I accidentally trying to tar one file created a file --one-file-system.
> 
> How do I delete without having rm think I'm specifying an option???

The one option for doing this that is most likely to be portable across
different UNIX-like systems would be prepending with a "./"

In this example, you'd do the following:

rm ./--one-file-system

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Thu Apr 24 14:02:32 2008
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Thu, 24 Apr 2008 14:02:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Deleting strange file name...
In-Reply-To: <ec0b5e080804241238n202de30br1af1f4965eca35c7@mail.gmail.com>
References: <20080423232138.pp3ya4wem80kwsw0@web.robinson-west.com>
	<20080424185541.GA19559@igor.rauhaus.net>
	<ec0b5e080804241238n202de30br1af1f4965eca35c7@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <200804241402.32709.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Daniel Johnson
# on Thursday 24 April 2008 12:38:

>> ?rm ./--one-file-system
>
>That would work in this example, but with the filename that starts
>with a pipe it wouldn't.

Those are different issues.  The '--blah' form is an argument to the 
program which suffers from no shell expansion but is likely to be 
treated as an option by the option parser unless prefixed with './' or 
preceded by a '--' argument.

Characters like  <,>,|,*,?,#,{},$,!,&,;,(),',",\, and ` have various 
meanings to the shell and result in expansion, interpolation, or other 
effects unless appropriately quoted or escaped.

The difference is seen in `echo \|` vs `echo --blah` -- the former is a 
matter of needing to escape the special meaning for the shell.  This is 
also why multi-argument system/exec is usually preferable to the single 
argument form (because the latter will fire-up a shell and parse the 
command as if you had typed it at a console.)

  perl -e 'system("echo", "|")'

  perl -e 'system("echo |")'

--Eric

From plug_1 at robinson-west.com  Thu Apr 24 14:57:13 2008
From: plug_1 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Thu, 24 Apr 2008 14:57:13 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Routing problem...
Message-ID: <20080424145713.dnejtikiyokogg48@web.robinson-west.com>

There is an FVX 538 VPN box that is replacing
my local area Linux based network.  Uge!  Okay,
how can I set things up so that people can stay
on my local area network without losing access
to the FVX 538???  Actually, I don't want access,
but everyone else does.  Sadly, every time someone
moves over to the FVX 538, a printer or other shared
resource disappears from the local area network.
This also makes backing machines up harder to do.
It's a netgear FVX 538.

|---|                 |---|
| G |------|          | X |
|---|      |          |---|
            |            |
            --------------------
|---|        |  | | | | | |
| W |---------  | | | | | |
|---|           | | | | | |

The above diagram shows the three
original servers hooked up to a
backbone switch.  People are pulling
off the switch and moving to the FVX
538 accordingly.  G for goose is the
mailhub/dhcp server.  W and X for web
and xerxes are the lan routers to the
Internet.

One thought is to hook the FVX 538 to a
new router, but how to get people who
want to go through it to route to it???

Another thought is to make that new router
a smart switch that replaces the backbone
switch.  I've never programmed a box to
do that.

I suppose one option is to statically
configure machines that want to go out
on the FVX 538 via a new router.

A possible option is to masquerade from
W or X onto the FVX 538, if that will work.

Adding another machine is not an ideal
solution.

While yes wine does appear to be better capable of running windows 
software at this time, if reactOS progresses far enough I see it as 
being potentially useful in getting people to use open source software. 
Many people don't know what open source really is and just think it's 
'amateur software that only geeks know how to use' not realizing that 
all the 'amateurs' in the world combined together can generally make a 
superior product then the big software companies. Generally once people 
start using open source and realize how good it is they are more likely 
to continue using it but it's hard for people to just immediately jump 
right into linux where everythings different and many of their most 
familiar applications (that they wasted good money on) won't work 
anymore. Also, I personally don't think any open source project is a 
waste. Take reactOS for example, even if the project were to die 
tomorrow for some reason alot of the code they write is merged into wine 
and their very existence improves the wine project. Thats one of the big 
strengths of open source compared to closed source. If a closed source 
project dies it is completely dead and was just a waste of time. If an 
open source project dies then it's code can be used be reused in a 
different project meaning even projects that don't complete their 
objectives still provide to the open source community.


Do not be afraid to joust a giant just because some people insist on believing in windmills.

From larry.brigman at gmail.com  Fri Apr 25 08:27:53 2008

Subject: [PLUG] What are .lock files
In-Reply-To: <20080424235449.27e6c45b.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080424235449.27e6c45b.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <ca5165530804250827m111ef5f3qe1b8403f013e902@mail.gmail.com>

On Thu, Apr 24, 2008 at 11:54 PM, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> Every time I try to do a backup it stops when it hits a .lock file.
>  This kills dead the idea of unattended backups. Using locate I find a
>  dozen of them on my hard disk.
>
>  It takes only 15 minutes to complete a backup of ~/, but it's annoying
>  to come back to the computer only to discover that the backup stopped
>  halfway through when it hit a .lock file.

If the .lock file exists, it there as a temporary file.  After a reboot, or
worst case after a restore, these files are of no value.  They are of
no value to the backup either.  Figure out a way to ignore the .lock files
in your backup.


From rsteff at comcast.net  Fri Apr 25 08:34:23 2008
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Fri, 25 Apr 2008 08:34:23 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] What are .lock files
In-Reply-To: <20080424235449.27e6c45b.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080424235449.27e6c45b.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <4811F9FF.7000904@comcast.net>

John Jason Jordan wrote:

> Every time I try to do a backup it stops when it hits a .lock file.

As I understand it, a lock file is used by a program to tell other
instances of that program, or other programs that it is using some
resource. You have a program, or programs that use .lock files for this
purpose.

Ordinarily, the program should clean up after itself and delete the lock
file when it is finished with it. If the program halts abnormally, the
lock file won't go away. At that point, the lock file is no longer
relevant, and may be deleted.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

From gently at gmail.com  Fri Apr 25 08:56:36 2008
From: gently at gmail.com (chris (fool) mccraw)
Date: Fri, 25 Apr 2008 08:56:36 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] What are .lock files
In-Reply-To: <20080424235449.27e6c45b.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080424235449.27e6c45b.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <ae2db4620804250856s4358dc4aodc8cd99395769eab@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/24/08, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> Every time I try to do a backup it stops when it hits a .lock file.
>  This kills dead the idea of unattended backups. Using locate I find a
>  dozen of them on my hard disk.
>
>  It takes only 15 minutes to complete a backup of ~/, but it's annoying
>  to come back to the computer only to discover that the backup stopped
>  halfway through when it hit a .lock file.

while file locking under unix is a hairy story
(http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File_locking#File_locking_in_UNIX),
backing up locks needs not be.  in fact, backing up a lock file really
shouldn't even hang--i'd be interested to see (the relevant portion
of) an strace of the backup process.  running as root should get every
file, regardless of type, if you use the right options (backing up
device files can be tricky if you're not careful.../dev/sda is a
really big file if you try to read its contents, and won't fit onto
/dev/sda1 without some compression =))

all that aside, if you (or your backup program) is using something like tar,

   tar --exclude=.lock

will dodge the problem.  if not using tar, generating a filelist and
then putting the files into the backup should somewhere, somewhen,
give you an opportunity to:

   grep -v /.lock$

luck++;

From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Apr 25 09:23:32 2008
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Fri, 25 Apr 2008 09:23:32 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] sudo to root question
In-Reply-To: <1209138514.19672.13.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <4811FA25.7040900@seitzassoc.com>
	<1209138514.19672.13.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.1.10.0804250910560.27302@guild.galois.com>

On Fri, 25 Apr 2008, Dan Roberts wrote:

> Hello All
>
> In the /etc/sudoers file snippet shown below...
>
> Could someone please explain the differences in consequence between 
> the two entries below..
>
> 1> droberts ALL = ALL
> OR
> 2> droberts ALL(ALL) = ALL
>
> I can't quite figure out what the difference in use or consequence 
> might be in either line..

I think the second one is illegal. It should read

   droberts  ALL = (ALL) ALL

The "(ALL)" is what sudo calls the Runas_Spec, which consists of a 
Runas_List in parens. By default, the Runas_List is "root," but it's 
configurable.

In the default configuration, there's not much practical difference 
between the two. In configuration #1, droberts would have to jump a 
hoop or two if he wanted to run a process as, e.g., the Apache user. 
Typically the hoop is navigated by using the su command:

   sudo su apache command

A legal version of configuration #2, otoh, would mean he could do it 
directly, e.g.,

   sudo -u apache command

The real power of the runas stuff becomes more apparent when you set 
runas_default to something other than root. In that case, you have to 
explicitly add root to your Runas_Spec.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/


From gently at gmail.com  Fri Apr 25 09:32:14 2008
From: gently at gmail.com (chris (fool) mccraw)
Date: Fri, 25 Apr 2008 09:32:14 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] sudo to root question

On 4/25/08, Dan Roberts <Daniel.G.Roberts at sanofi-aventis.com> wrote:
> Hello All
>
>  In the /etc/sudoers file snippet shown below...
>  Could someone please explain the differences in consequence between the
>  two entries below..
>
>  1> droberts ALL = ALL
>  OR
>  2> droberts ALL(ALL) = ALL

as i understand from the sudoers(5) man page and a bit of testing, the
(ALL) is what userid's the user on the left is allowed to run the
commands on the right as.

ALL is pretty self explanatory, but if it's absent, it actually
creates the restriction that the authorized user can run commands ONLY
as root, and not as, say, www-data or any other userid.

of course, in the case of ALL = ALL, it's not changing anything, since
you can always sudo su - otheruser and do the same thing...

From gently at gmail.com  Fri Apr 25 10:34:52 2008
From: gently at gmail.com (chris (fool) mccraw)
Date: Fri, 25 Apr 2008 10:34:52 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] sudo to root question
In-Reply-To: <1209144037.19672.19.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <4811FA25.7040900@seitzassoc.com>
	<1209138514.19672.13.camel@localhost.localdomain>
	<alpine.OSX.1.10.0804250910560.27302@guild.galois.com>
	<1209144037.19672.19.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <ae2db4620804251034l4f1ad80eue35b73f97511c5c8@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/25/08, Dan Roberts <Daniel.G.Roberts at sanofi-aventis.com> wrote:
> Thanks for the reply and note on my miss-configuration!
>  Might anyone be willing to offer what "root" like sudo commands you may
>  allow your power users to perform?
>  I am getting shredded on trying to implement a policy that says no one
>  is to have full sudo to root access..But I am also arguing that it is a
>  reasonable approach to all specific sudo to root acccess to a LIMITED
>  command subset..
>  What might be those command subset in your experience that seems
>  reasonable?

in my experience, it's really hard to securely set up sudo so that
people can't subvert it--a few simple commands are easy enough
(shutdown, ls), but once you get into anything complicated or
explicitly excluding commands, it doesn't take much for a clever
person to get a root shell (most editors all you to shell out;
disincluding shells is great until i copy a shell into a binary called
"notashell" and run that instead; a surprising number of innocuous
commands can be used to subvert the system---think cat /etc/shadow and
then running crack on it).

in the past, i've taken a functional view on this--what is it you
*actually need to do* and then crafting access methods that give the
minimum privilege needed to accomplish the task.  it usually comes
down to group ownership/permissions on certain files, rather than
sudo, and i usually sleep decently at night knowing that they aren't
going to have done something naughty in the interim.

unfortunately cranky power users are the hardest to pin down, but if
you have buy-in on the policy and can inconvenience them a little into
waiting until they run up against something they can't do and having
you enable that (after they justify their need!), you have a much
better likelihood (IMHO) of keeping them from shooting you in the
foot.

luck++;

complex. I've also used tar and dar, but I don't like my backups

KISS.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Apr 25 10:48:33 2008
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Fri, 25 Apr 2008 10:48:33 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] sudo to root question
In-Reply-To: <1209144037.19672.19.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <4811FA25.7040900@seitzassoc.com>
	<1209138514.19672.13.camel@localhost.localdomain>
	<alpine.OSX.1.10.0804250910560.27302@guild.galois.com>
	<1209144037.19672.19.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.1.10.0804251033420.27302@guild.galois.com>

On Fri, 25 Apr 2008, Dan Roberts wrote:

> Might anyone be willing to offer what "root" like sudo commands you 
> may allow your power users to perform?
>
> I am getting shredded on trying to implement a policy that says no 
> one is to have full sudo to root access..But I am also arguing that 
> it is a reasonable approach to all specific sudo to root acccess to 
> a LIMITED command subset..
>
> What might be those command subset in your experience that seems 
> reasonable?

The real question is the trust you have for your users. If you don't 
trust them, don't give them sudo with any root privileges.

Run as uid 0 (root), a large percentage of command-line utilities can 
undermine a system. E.g., say you want to allow some users to run 
"less" so they can view log files or other protected configurations. 
Well, less can be used to launch an editor (press 'v' to try it 
yourself). So anyone with access to "less /etc/sudoers" will be able 
to edit the file!

File utils (cp, mv, and ln) or stream editors (awk, sed) are just as 
easily abused.

I'm not saying that a limited sudo set isn't a good idea. It can allow 
conscientious users to care for themselves *and* stay out of harm's 
way.

Start with the trust question, however, because any root access is a 
wide-open door for a malicious or too-inquisitive user.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Fri Apr 25 11:34:56 2008
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Fri, 25 Apr 2008 11:34:56 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] sudo to root question

References: <4811FA25.7040900@seitzassoc.com>	<1209138514.19672.13.camel@localhost.localdomain>	<alpine.OSX.1.10.0804250910560.27302@guild.galois.com>	<1209144037.19672.19.camel@localhost.localdomain>
	<ae2db4620804251034l4f1ad80eue35b73f97511c5c8@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <48122450.2080300@nakedape.cc>

chris (fool) mccraw wrote:

> in my experience, it's really hard to securely set up sudo so that
> people can't subvert it--a few simple commands are easy enough
> (shutdown, ls), but once you get into anything complicated or
> explicitly excluding commands, it doesn't take much for a clever
> person to get a root shell (most editors all you to shell out;

Newer versions of sudo have a program and rule called 'sudoedit', which 
gets around the editor hole. It:
   1. Makes a copy of the file to edit in /tmp
   2. Runs $EDITOR on the file as the user
   3. Copies the file in place when the editor exits

OTOH there are still plenty of seemingly innocuous ways to get in, like 
"less".

Wil

John Jason Jordan wrote:

> Because of my simple needs I use Nautilus to drag ~/ on my laptop to a
> folder on my desktop computer. That's where I run into the .lock
> problem.

I don't understand how these files cause Nautilus to "lock". I have 
never seen it do anything like that. Does it prompt you when it stops? 
There is something about dragging and dropping large quantities of files 
that always makes me a little nervous... I am clumsy enough that it 
seems far too easy to let go at the wrong moment and have things start 
going into totally the wrong place.

Since you're doing this between computers, are you using Nautilus to 
copy these files over a network?

> Today I decided to try the cp command instead, but cp won't
> copy directories unless I can figure out the command, and the man page
> eludes my comprehension. 

Well, I guess you have to know that by default SOURCE can only be a 
file, not a directory and I guess the man page doesn't mention that 
directly. If the man page is too terse, though, you will find that such 
basic UNIX commands are documented in friendlier terms all over the web. 
Any college with UNIX or Linux will have some; try our own for example:

	http://www.cat.pdx.edu/unix/the-unix-file-system.html

In fact,  I would suggest if the topics covered on that page are 
unfamiliar to you that you spend some time reviewing it; it's pretty 
fundamental stuff. It is a testament to how well developed our desktop 
interfaces are that you have been able to get along this far without it, 
but it also explains many of the troubles you have. Spending time 
learning core UNIX stuff like this will make your life much easier.

> Today I wanted to make a more thorough backup in anticipation of doing
> an apt-get dist-upgrade to Hardy. I decided to try to rsync to an empty
> folder on the desktop. It worked great for ~/, but when I tried other
> folders I got a lot of errors - permission denied, even when I sudo'ed
> it. From past experience, rsync is a pain. Lots of bugs, and horribly
> complex.

rsync is indeed a complex tool; there are subtleties that are easy to 
miss, like the difference in including a trailing slash in the source 
directory or not. But it isn't especially buggy; aside from an 
occasional cryptic errors when rsyncing with remote servers that I have 
no access to (which, it seems, turns out to be the result of system 
load), every error I have run into has been of the PEBKAC sort.

Wil

Wil

On Fri, Apr 25, 2008 at 11:34 AM, Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc> wrote:
>  Newer versions of sudo have a program and rule called 'sudoedit', which
> gets around the editor hole. It:
>   1. Makes a copy of the file to edit in /tmp
>   2. Runs $EDITOR on the file as the user
>   3. Copies the file in place when the editor exits

Beware -- sudoedit doesn't seem to do any form of file locking, so the
last editor to exit gets the final say.

Does anyone know if/how sudo determines that a given comand is
actually the one that should be allowed? (Say I have root access to
ls, I add "." to my PATH, then create a copy of /bin/bash that's
called /tmp/ls, cd to /tmp and run 'sudo ls')

	<48122450.2080300@nakedape.cc>
	<c834ec80804251339y51ab086cr3cb6b7f19985073f@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.1.10.0804251348420.27302@guild.galois.com>

On Fri, 25 Apr 2008, Rogan Creswick wrote:

> Does anyone know if/how sudo determines that a given comand is 
> actually the one that should be allowed? (Say I have root access to 
> ls, I add "." to my PATH, then create a copy of /bin/bash that's 
> called /tmp/ls, cd to /tmp and run 'sudo ls')

sudoers takes program arguments with full paths only:

     A Cmnd_List is a list of one or more commandnames, directories,
     and other aliases.  A commandname is a fully qualified filename
     which may include shell-style wildcards (see the Wildcards section
     below). [...] When you specify a directory in a a Cmnd_List, the
     user will be able to run any file within that directory (but not
     in any subdirectories therein).

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/

On 4/25/08, Rogan Creswick <creswick at gmail.com> wrote:

>  Does anyone know if/how sudo determines that a given comand is
>  actually the one that should be allowed? (Say I have root access to
>  ls, I add "." to my PATH, then create a copy of /bin/bash that's
>  called /tmp/ls, cd to /tmp and run 'sudo ls')

it tries to be smart even when you're not, but really, you need to
specify fully qualified commands in /etc/sudoers to have any hope
of things working out the way you (as an access-restricting sysadmin)
would want.  except, apparently, in old debian.

the SECURITY NOTES section of the sudo man page will say the rest
better than i can.

the following:

'cd /this/is/the/source/directory; find . | cpio -pdm /this/is/the/ 
destination/directory/'

Others will mention theirs, I'm sure.

From galens at seitzassoc.com  Sat Apr 26 11:41:25 2008
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)
Date: Sat, 26 Apr 2008 11:41:25 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] apache auth based on uri
In-Reply-To: <4811FA25.7040900@seitzassoc.com>
References: <4811FA25.7040900@seitzassoc.com>
Message-ID: <48137755.8000205@seitzassoc.com>

For the record, here's the answer I received from the apache user 
mailing list.

galen


From: Nick Kew
Subject: Re: auth based on uri
Newsgroups: gmane.comp.apache.user
Date: 2008-04-25 23:03:20 GMT (19 hours and 21 minutes ago)

On Fri, 25 Apr 2008 21:20:35 +0000 (UTC)
Galen Seitz <galens <at> seitzassoc.com> wrote:

 > Is it possible to do authorization based on a pattern in the uri?
 > I'd like to be able to create a single container that would control
 > access to various directories where the directory name is the same
 > as an auth group name.  For example:
 >
 > <DirectoryMatch /share/([^/]+) >
 >     ...
 >     Require group \1
 > </DirectoryMatch>

No.

With Apache 2.2 or lower, the best you can do is use
mod_rewrite to map from / or /group/ or (your choice)
to an appropriate directory based on REMOTE_USER
with a "Require valid-user".

In future versions, we hope to offer better options.

 > <Directory /share/proj1 >
 >     ...
 >     Require group proj1
 > </Directory>
 >
 > <Directory /share/proj2 >
 >     ...
 >     Require group proj2
 > </Directory>

You can also use mod_macro to take the pain out of that.

Subject: [PLUG] Setting serial parameters

Help.  I am trying to communicate with a CP-290, and X10 controller.  I did this
last year, so I know it is possible.  Here is a summary of the status.

Required: Baud rate : 600, Data bits : 8, Parity : None, Stop bits : 1

setserial says:
  setserial /dev/ttyS1 -a
/dev/ttyS1, Line 1, UART: 16550A, Port: 0x02f8, IRQ: 3
         Baud_base: 115200, close_delay: 50, divisor: 192
         closing_wait: 3000
         Flags: spd_cust skip_test

Running x10 -t says it will take about 10 seconds, but it runs forever. ^c to stop.

So the first thing I would like to determine and possibly change are the serial
parameters.  Is the use of spd_cust and divisor the way to set the baud down to
600?  How do I set the other parameters?

Running Kubuntu 7.04 (Feisty Fawn)

From rsteff at comcast.net  Sat Apr 26 13:27:49 2008
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Sat, 26 Apr 2008 13:27:49 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Setting serial parameters
In-Reply-To: <48138567.3000702@dslnorthwest.net>
References: <48138567.3000702@dslnorthwest.net>
Message-ID: <48139045.2010506@comcast.net>

Denis Heidtmann wrote:
> Help.  I am trying to communicate with a CP-290, and X10 controller.  I did this
> last year, so I know it is possible.  Here is a summary of the status.

Are you attempting to communicate with the CP-290 through a program you
are writing, or are you using someone else's program?

I have a CP-290 that I use with a program called X10. I don't recall
where I got it, but it came as X10.tar.gz. The program takes care of
setting up the parameters, which are a bit strange. Apparently this
program has appropriate delays built into it's serial port
communications subroutine.

I remember reading something about the old DOS program that came with
the CP-290. It said that there was a special timing requirement that
would not work with Windows. I don't know why, but it appears to be
true. I was never able to get the included program working under Win95
or Win98.

X10, the Linux program, works fine. I had to set up a configuration file
and then run the usual make process. It's still available for download from:

ftp://ftp.foxharp.boston.ma.us/pub/pgf/x10/


-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

Denis Heidtmann wrote:

> The program I am using is x10 (lower case). 

Now that I pay attention when I look at it, the program I use is also
spelled in lower case.

> It is written by Aaron Hightower. 

Mine is a revision by Paul Fox of a program by Larry Campbell.

> You will note that I mentioned running x10 -t, the self-test option. 

Mine doesn't have that as an option. It has a similar one:

rsteff at Moonguide:~> x10 -h
Command error: Invalid command name
Usage:
<...>
x10 diagnostic              run interface self-test diagnostics
<...>

All of the options for my version start with x10, and are followed by a
word or words, but no dash.

> I have 
> been assuming that setting up the serial is the problem.  I do not think that 
> the x10 program is the problem.  It ran last year and the year before, after 
> finding the correct serial port.  If I have the wrong serial port, it tells me 
> it cannot communicate with the CP-290.

Do you have the source code? Perhaps you could search it for what it
wants in the way of a serial port. I'm not a C programmer, but I can
sort of follow what's going on when I read the file that has the code to
access the serial port.

> I am interested in your mention of a configuration file.  I assume that is for 
> compiling, not for running?

Yes. I run my with CRONTAB entries.

My file, x10config, contains the following:
---------------------------------------------------------------------------
#
# this file should contain x10 appliance aliases, one per line, as:
#   appliance-name  housecode  modulenumber
# for example:
#  mydesklamp	A	4
#  atticfan	B	3
#
# In addition, the devicename for the line the Powerhouse is attached to may
# be specified with:
#  TTY	/dev/tty00
# and the default housecode with:
#  HOUSECODE	A
# this is only used by the switches on the unit itself, and units
#  specified by number only on the command line.
#
LR-AcornLts	K 1
BR-GentsChest	K 3
LR-TableLmp	K 7
BR-PictureLmp	K 8
#
#
# Oregon City:
LATITUDE	45:34
LONGITUDE	-122.57
#
# set default housecode -- the one the switches will use
HOUSECODE	K
TTY		/dev/ttyX10
#TTY		annex:7002
---------------------------------------------------------------------------

If I ever re-compile it I'll just use /dev/ttyS0 for TTY. I have to
re-create a sym link after every boot to get it to work. I finally
figured out how to do that with an entry in /etc/init.d/boot.local

# create a symlink for the X10 program
ln -s /dev/ttyS0 /dev/ttyX10

I suspect that is not particularly relevant to your program, but maybe
it will trigger a thought.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

Performing self-test (This will take about 10 seconds)

From kens at cad2cam.com  Sun Apr 27 10:34:16 2008
From: kens at cad2cam.com (Ken Stephens)
Date: Sun, 27 Apr 2008 10:34:16 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Deleting strange file name...
In-Reply-To: <200804241402.32709.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <20080423232138.pp3ya4wem80kwsw0@web.robinson-west.com>	<20080424185541.GA19559@igor.rauhaus.net>	<ec0b5e080804241238n202de30br1af1f4965eca35c7@mail.gmail.com>
	<200804241402.32709.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
Message-ID: <4814B918.6000809@cad2cam.com>

Eric Wilhelm wrote:
> # from Daniel Johnson
> # on Thursday 24 April 2008 12:38:
>
>   
>>>  rm ./--one-file-system
>>>       
>> That would work in this example, but with the filename that starts
>> with a pipe it wouldn't.
>>     
>
> Those are different issues.  The '--blah' form is an argument to the 
> program which suffers from no shell expansion but is likely to be 
> treated as an option by the option parser unless prefixed with './' or 
> preceded by a '--' argument.
>
> Characters like  <,>,|,*,?,#,{},$,!,&,;,(),',",\, and ` have various 
> meanings to the shell and result in expansion, interpolation, or other 
> effects unless appropriately quoted or escaped.
>
> The difference is seen in `echo \|` vs `echo --blah` -- the former is a 
> matter of needing to escape the special meaning for the shell.  This is 
> also why multi-argument system/exec is usually preferable to the single 
> argument form (because the latter will fire-up a shell and parse the 
> command as if you had typed it at a console.)
>
>   perl -e 'system("echo", "|")'
>
>   perl -e 'system("echo |")'
>
> --Eric
>   
I am really surprised no one suggested using the old standby command: find.

find . -name "-i" -print exec rm -i {} \;

No there is a real geek's solution.

Ken

On Sat, 2008-04-26 at 18:25 -0700, Denis Heidtmann wrote:

> The docs for my program mention the symlink to set the serial port, as well as 
> setting an environment variable X10_PORTNAME.  I chose the latter.  But here is 
> what I get with various settings:
> 
> 
> parents at R2D2:~$ X10_PORTNAME=/dev/ttys0
> parents at R2D2:~$ x10 -t
> Could not access serial port /dev/ttys0
> Please set environment variable X10_PORTNAME to proper serial device
> or else make the appropriate soft link as superuser.

You have to export the environment variable for processes other than the
current shell to see it:

$ X10_PORTNAME=/dev/ttyS0
$ export X10_PORTNAME

or just:

$ export X10_PORTNAME=/dev/ttyS0

Wil

From ncwalker at gmail.com  Mon Apr 28 23:40:56 2008
From: ncwalker at gmail.com (Nye Walker)
Date: Mon, 28 Apr 2008 23:40:56 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] '98 Network Card Too Old?
In-Reply-To: <4816C15E.100@comcast.net>
References: <4816C15E.100@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <99cab65c0804282340i3cf92bc2u852efcf82dcfed2c@mail.gmail.com>

Comcast itself isn't a good reason to upgrade from the card, as the transfer
rates don't come close to utilizing the full speed of that card.  How much
internal transfer do you do at your location?  If you did a lot of
computer-to-computer or computer-to-nas as I do, a gigabit card might be
worth the cost.

It is more than adequate for Comcasts fastest speeds though, if that is your
concern.

From ncwalker at gmail.com  Tue Apr 29 04:41:36 2008
From: ncwalker at gmail.com (Nye Walker)

Subject: [PLUG] '98 Network Card Too Old?

	<99cab65c0804282340i3cf92bc2u852efcf82dcfed2c@mail.gmail.com>
	<4816C5CF.6080008@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <99cab65c0804290441r76bff53djbd6fa87c04b57097@mail.gmail.com>

I don't know about other's experiences, but I have had NICs wear out.
Generally they are cheaper manufacturers (generally corrosion on the pins).
It is rare though.

If you start noticing packet loss, try replacing the cable you are using, if
that doesn't work, inspect the RJ45 jack on the network card for any signs
of pin damage (either bent of corroded).
