From heinlein at madboa.com  Tue Apr 29 08:22:39 2008
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Tue, 29 Apr 2008 08:22:39 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] SSL Certs
In-Reply-To: <4816CD1D.8030008@gmail.com>
References: <4816CD1D.8030008@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.1.10.0804290807280.702@guild.galois.com>

On Tue, 29 Apr 2008, Sean Whitney wrote:

> I'm looking for recommendations on where to purchase a SSL cert for 
> our small business.
>
> I bought one before through godaddy and I wasn't really impressed 
> with the "chaining" antics of what you purchase.
>
> Preferable this would be a "wildcard" cert that could be applied to 
> any subdomain.
>
> I'm looking for recommendations based on
>
> 1) price
> 2) quality of support
> 3) do no evil business ethics.

There are two issues here:

1. You don't mention what level of trust you want the certificate
    to inspire in your customers.

2. The number of independent certificate authorities (CAs) has
    dropped recently because VeriSign has been buying all its
    competitors.

If you just want a cert signed by a CA that has its root certificates 
in most browsers, RapidSSL.com is fine. You can get a single-host cert 
for $69 or a wilcard cert for $199.

RapidSSL certs are merely "domain control validated," however, meaning 
that all RapidSSL guarantees is that you're pretty likely to own the 
domain advertised in your cert. That's probably good enough for most 
uses, but you're the one who has to make the decision. Also, RapidSSL 
was purchased by VeriSign a couple years ago.

If you're dealing with customers with stricter needs, then you'll 
probably have to find a CA that does something resembling real due 
diligence to verify your identity. That probably means a 
VeriSign-branded certificate.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/


From qhartman at gmail.com  Tue Apr 29 08:36:27 2008
From: qhartman at gmail.com (Quentin Hartman)

>
> I use Cepstral's "Text To Speech" (TTS) engine to generate messages for
> use by Asterisk.  Though the engine and selected voice works very well

> probably I didn't recognize it for what it is.
>

>

First I think you need to better define what you mean by "smooth". Are there
pitch problems? Cadence problems? Inflection problems? If Cepstrals supports
mark-up to adjust pronunciation I'd attack it from that direction, you're
likely to have much better results. Manipulating audio is definitely an art.
I doubt you will find anything that  will automagically do what you are
after, unless there are clear and well defined aberrations that are simple
to isolate. It doesn't sound like that's the case though.

-Regards-

-Quentin Hartman-

Paul Heinlein wrote:
> If you just want a cert signed by a CA that has its root certificates 
> in most browsers, RapidSSL.com is fine. You can get a single-host cert 
> for $69 or a wilcard cert for $199.

Also keep in mind the wildcard cert from RapidSSL for $199 can only be tied to 
one key.  This is fine for IP-based virtualhosts, but is riskier for SSL 
services spread out over a bunch of servers.  A single server compromise and all 
your services are at risk.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Apr 30 00:53:50 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 30 Apr 2008 00:53:50 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Speech recognition on Linux
Message-ID: <20080430075350.GE31240@gate.kl-ic.com>


My wife is spending about $8000 a year on medical transcription, and
is hearing encouraging things about the proprietary program "Dragon
Naturally Speaking" (D.N.S.).  The medical version is $1200 (yikes).  
Sadly, there does not appear to be any Linux software in the same
league, though progress is being made and I expect things to get
much better in a year or two.

We run a nearly pure Linux shop, with a few Windoze tools running
under VMware.  I would rather not run any native Windows machines.

There are reports that D.N.S. can be cobbled into Wine, so we can
run it under Linux.  If anybody has experience with that, I would
like to hear about it.  We would also like to work out some kind
of sharing setup so she can use it at both her office and at home,
perhaps by moving sound data over the VPN link (60msec ping time
through Seattle) or by buying 2 copies ( 2*yikes !!!) and sharing
the config and training files over the network.  Of course, security
and patient confidentiality are paramount;  I doubt we could achieve
sufficient security with a Windows machine in the mix.

If you have knowledge of moving clean sound over that much latency,
please share it;  we would rather buy a single copy of D.N.S. 

BTW, the stories you hear about "rich doctors" are about as truthful
as the 1930's stories about "rich Jews".  Most doctors work long hours
for lower wages than most technical professionals.  Spending $2400
*hurts*.  Not as much as spending $8000/yr, granted, but there will
be staff costs associated with cleaning up the inevitable speech
recognition mistakes, so it may prove to be an expensive mistake.

Keith

From qhartman at gmail.com  Wed Apr 30 06:32:33 2008
From: qhartman at gmail.com (Quentin Hartman)
Date: Wed, 30 Apr 2008 06:32:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Smoothing audio
In-Reply-To: <48174E73.80304@acm.org>
References: <48173C8C.5040506@acm.org>
	<8975a95f0804290836w4c93fe6ew4c52b4f2d5976803@mail.gmail.com>
	<48174E73.80304@acm.org>
Message-ID: <8975a95f0804300632l5ca42da7jda770a2664b68b1d@mail.gmail.com>

On Tue, Apr 29, 2008 at 9:36 AM, Roderick A. Anderson <raanders at acm.org>
wrote:

> Thanks for the clue-stick tap.  :-)


My pleasure. Clue-sticking is one of my favorite hobbies. :D


>  I listened to the files again and
> realized it was more a phone system issue.  Could be my home phone, cell
> phone or maybe even the Asterisk box is causing the problems.  Most of
> the choppy pieces were not so bad when played on my workstation.


I presume that you are feeding the audio in from output on a sound card?
Make sure the gain is not too high. If the bad parts tend to be at louder
points or "s" or "t" sounds in the speech, your telephony card might be
clipping due to too high volume. Does your analog interface card do any sort
of audio processing in hardware? If so, try turning that on/off and see if
it makes any difference. Finally, make sure whatever voice settings you are
using are not producing any weird high or low-pitched tones. Phone systems
are really only meant to carry frequencies found in normal speech, and
adding frequencies outside of that range can sound really awful.

-- 
-Regards-

-Quentin Hartman-

If running in a VM is an option, then maybe you could put it in its own
VM and move it back and forth on its own USB stick/drive, so you have to
pay for only one license. You could encrypt the drive to protect the
data and remove NICs from the VM so it is isolated from the rest of the
world. Getting data in and out of it then becomes an exercise in local
storage manipulation.

> Hello:
> I often have programs that operate a server and one or more clients on a
> local network. I have always had a data file on my server with the
> address at which my server is to listen (the port is hard coded). When I
> move the program to another computer, I need to change my IP address
> data file. I would like to modify my program to get the IP address of
> it's host not knowing the host name. Any ideas?
> Thanks,
> Wayne
>

If you want something that works most of the time then just grab the IP
address of eth0 from "ifconfig eth0" output. If you want something that
works all of the time, well, that can be surprisingly tricky, say if
there is no eth0 or if it's not configured. And even if eth0 is present
and configured, it may have been intended that eth1 have the primary
IP address for the host?

This topic came up on the kernel list a couple years ago and it turns
out there is no universal answer to knowing which IP address of possibly
several is meant to be primary IP address for a host.

Terry



From plug at the-wes.com  Wed Apr 30 10:23:26 2008
From: plug at the-wes.com (wes)
Date: Wed, 30 Apr 2008 10:23:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] getting IP address of host
In-Reply-To: <48167E26.9070409@pacifier.com>
References: <48167E26.9070409@pacifier.com>
Message-ID: <b399d1c10804301023i298857c6i5dc142a3cfb2af8c@mail.gmail.com>

My understanding of your question is:

* You have an application running in a client-server model
* The server component is running on one server machine
* Client components are running on any number of workstations
* You want to be able to move the application's server component around to
different machines at will
* You want the clients to learn on which machine the server is running
automatically

I would use DNS to accomplish this. I would create a DNS hostname
specifically for the program, and set its A record to the IP of the current
server machine. Then just have the clients connect to the hostname, and it
will use the workstation's DNS resolver to look up the IP on demand.

Another way of doing this would be to use a network broadcast request. The
client would send a request to the network broadcast address, and the server
would pick up on the message and respond with its IP. The client would then
establish its application protocol connection using this IP.

-wes

From timothy.selivanow at virtualxistenz.com  Wed Apr 30 10:49:15 2008
From: timothy.selivanow at virtualxistenz.com (Timothy Selivanow)

On Mon, 2008-04-28 at 18:47 -0700, Wayne E. Van Loon Sr. wrote:
> Hello:
> I often have programs that operate a server and one or more clients on a 
> local network. I have always had a data file on my server with the 
> address at which my server is to listen (the port is hard coded). When I 
> move the program to another computer, I need to change my IP address 
> data file. I would like to modify my program to get the IP address of 
> it's host not knowing the host name. Any ideas?
> Thanks,
> Wayne

Most languages should have the gethostent() suite of functions.  You can
look at `man 3 gethostent` for a quick look.  Most languages just access
the C library to do this (I know Perl does) and wraps it in their
specific style.


--Tim

On Wed, 2008-04-30 at 10:00 -0700, Paul Heinlein wrote:

> > You should be able to parse it from the stdout of /sbin/ifconfig. I 
> > don't have a system with 2 NIC's, so I don't know how much 
> > complexity that would introduce (in the event that's a concern for 
> > you).
> 
> Or from /sbin/ip, e.g.,
> 
>    ip addr show eth0 | awk '/inet / {print $2}' | cut -d/ -f1
> 

Even better would be to choose the interface that is the default
gateway:

ip route ls|awk '/^default/ {print $5}'|xargs ip addr show|awk '/inet / {print $2}' | cut -d/ -f1

From garton.tim at gmail.com  Wed Apr 30 12:24:40 2008
From: garton.tim at gmail.com (Tim Garton)
Date: Wed, 30 Apr 2008 12:24:40 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] getting IP address of host
In-Reply-To: <48167E26.9070409@pacifier.com>
References: <48167E26.9070409@pacifier.com>
Message-ID: <8fcf3b560804301224h45950b56oc01d1ef087422d2a@mail.gmail.com>

Is there some reason you want the server piece to bind to a specific
IP?  Most languages/network libraries will allow you to either bind to
all IPs or bind to "0.0.0.0" which will cause the server to listen on
all interfaces/all IPs.  If you're worried about security you can then
use iptables to block incoming requests to IPs you don't want to
service, but it sounds like you are running this on a local network so
it wouldn't be an issue anyway.

Tim

On Wed, 2008-04-30 at 20:47 -0700, Luke Eckley wrote:
> Hans Reiser, creator of the ReiserFS filesystem, has just been found
> guilty of first degree murder.
> 
> Here is the URL.
> <http://blog.wired.com/27bstroke6/2008/04/reiser-guilty-o.html>
> 

If Nina is found alive...  

I'm ashamed of the jurors, there doesn't seem to be any solid 
evidence to prove that Nina died let alone that there was a 
murder.  The whole thing stinks.

I guess the moral of the trial is, don't act guilty even if 
all the evidence against you is circumstantial.  Hans shouldn't 
have admitted to evading the police.  I will say, it's dangerous
to speak on your own behalf in a court room because it's easy to
make a bad impression.

25 years for a murder case where there is no body and no murder
weapon seems extreme to say the least.  Statutes that allow
murder convictions on circumstantial evidence alone should be 
revised.  What kind of jury convicts based solely on it's
collective opinion of someone with no hard evidence to back 
that opinion up?  I hope that Hans will file for an appeal.

It's hard to believe reading the linked to site that this is
for real.

    --  Michael Robinson
